A Person's Failure (Continued) 229

— Shu Mang

Thus, his youthful dreams ended; he became indifferent to the many windy and rainy days of unhappiness that followed. He diligently worked "like a beast" for the Third Master for twelve years, earning a bit of meager land, burying his dead parents and wife, and then lived on like a fool. However, he had hope: he hoped that one day good fortune would fall upon him, and then everything would go smoothly, "with land, money, and good days..."

In famine years, the young men all went to work in distant cities, but the young "Fifth Uncle" (at that time, people only called him "A-Wu") still hid in the countryside and complained about his fate, selling his only two-plus acres of land. Afterwards, he continued to do the "fine work" he was content with.

Thus, year after year passed, and the young men from the distant cities returned. Several years of labor and earnings brought considerable wealth, so they bought land, bought houses, and built their lives in stability; they went to see "Brother A-Wu" of the same generation, who was already the gray-haired Fifth Uncle. He was still cultivating laboriously, envying others' "fate," hoping that one day good fortune would fall upon him...

Just like that, lightly, wastefully, and unconsciously, Fifth Uncle's life passed tragically: now, at dusk or dawn, in moments of loneliness, he realized that good fortune might never come; he waited a lifetime, hoped a lifetime, but the result was a "failure."

Fifth Uncle thought of the grave, thought of death—he dared not think anymore.

He admitted that he was a failure in life.

On the Boat 230

— Li Xuan

The river water gurgled, and the lights on both banks gradually faded and went out with the disappearance of dusk. A dim red river lantern hung by the bridgehead, gently swayed by the wind, its red flame flickering like a ghost fire. The entire wilderness was still, and the thick black night air spread like a mist, drifting along the river's body into the vast fields. Under the clear blue sky, it was a charming quiet night.

To the left of the bridge arch, a yellow punt lay askew. A wide gangplank was set up from the bow of the boat to the shore. A man slowly walked out of the village, swaying his body. He crossed a stretch of sinking sand. As he stepped onto the gangplank, a woman crawled out of the mat shed in the stern. She held a small Mobil lamp in her hand. She stood up, and the light flickered twice in the river wind. The man also bumped into her. The woman hunched down again and crawled back into the shed, saying:

"You're back so late, look how drunk you are."

The man didn't speak. He stuffed an empty grain sack under the deck board, and then flipped over and lay down like a lump of mud, drowsily squinting his eyes, nodding his head as if he was about to fall asleep. The woman sat next to him, pushing his flushed, greasy face, and asked:

"How much money did you sell it for, you dead pig? You haven't even mixed in the sand yet. What will you do if you fall asleep?"

He remembered mixing in the sand, and seemed to sober up a lot. The grain store owner's face had always been frightening. He remembered that the last time he sold a bag of wheat, after the grain was all transferred from the boat to the shore, Hongchang Store discovered that eight pecks were missing. Without saying a word, they called two policemen to arrest him and take him to the station. Only after his wife handed over all the money she had saved did they release him, and they didn't even give him a penny for boat fare.

He held up five fingers, telling the woman the amount of money he had sold this bag of sorghum for today. He pulled back his arm, grabbed the tile pot by the quilt, and drank for a while. Then, he suddenly darted to the front cabin, where a fellow was sleeping soundly. He leaned down and punched him hard. The fellow grabbed the quilt and cursed unclearly:

"Damn your sister, you drank enough cat pee, why don't you go find a woman? What are you doing hitting me in the middle of the night?"

"Hey, get up, we haven't mixed in the sand yet! Tomorrow, we'll see the patrolmen come to arrest people, and I'll have them arrest you!"

This fellow, who was half-asleep, sat up, put on his clothes, and rubbed his eyes with his big fist, saying:

"Anyway, we didn't steal, we didn't break the law, damn you. You should be sent to the Public Security Bureau!"

"Are you dividing the money or not? If you don't, all the trouble will be mine..."

The two men cursed each other. One after the other, they walked down the boat along the gangplank. Each of them carried a tin dustpan, shoveled full of sand on the beach, and then climbed back onto the boat, pouring it into the hold filled with sorghum. After each person carried five loads, they returned to the boat, grabbed a small wooden board, jumped into the sorghum hold, and tried their best to spread the sand particles, mixing it with the sorghum so that it was indistinguishable. They thinly scattered the three bags of sorghum that were placed on the hold cover on the surface, and everything was properly cleaned up. The two fellows wiped the sweat from their faces and squatted side by side on the riverbank to wash their faces. The drunken man turned back to the stern and slept with the woman in his arms. The other sat on the bow of the boat, lonely, holding a pipe, the firelight flashing red in the night shadows. He was used to the leisurely sound of the water on this small river, and he was also familiar with this quiet night. Everything was as before. The man who slept with the woman in his arms snored very loudly. He thought that was happy and blissful, especially on this spring evening. Finally, he thought about the bag of sorghum his fellow stole. Tomorrow, they would divide the money. After getting the money, he would run to the town in the evening and find a "half-covered door" to sleep with her for a night, wouldn't that be happy too?...

Looking up at the sky, three stars hung obliquely in the blue sky. He tapped off the ash from his pipe and climbed back into the cabin to lie down.

Village Fair 231

— Wen

A-Gen and A-Mao were just darting around in the crowd.

The two eleven- or twelve-year-old children were wearing single shirts, and their faces were still flushed. After all, it was the end of March, and children love excitement. Bean-sized beads of sweat were flowing on A-Gen's forehead, and he didn't wipe them with his sleeves. His sleeves were already greasy enough. A-Mao was also wiping, but he was wiping snot, not sweat.

More and more people were gathering on that street. Everyone was looking outside the city. The mothers and daughters were leaning against the windows along the street, some standing at the entrances of the shops. The people in the shops were also craning their necks, not doing business. The sugarcane stalls at the intersection were busy scraping the skin, and there were strings of peeled water chestnuts, tenderly enticing the fairgoers who were thirsty for excitement.

"It's coming, it's coming!"

The crowd stirred, but there was actually nothing there. Everyone was patient. They often grabbed a familiar passerby. "How far is the fair from there?", "It's coming, it's coming" was usually the answer.

A-Gen and A-Mao were small, so they squeezed to a good spot at a corner. But they were short, so they had to look up and stand on tiptoes. Still, they could only see the broad backs of others. They were anxiously darting around, their liver-like red faces covered in snot and sweat.

Many people had already stood on the short railings of the rice shop at the corner, craning their necks. A-Gen climbed up and sat on it, and A-Mao followed A-Gen's example. So, they sat on the short railings and waited very proudly.

"The fair is coming, the fair is coming!" Another uproar.

They waited for almost three li.

"Gong! Gong!" The sound of gongs vibrated down the street from afar.

Slowly, the small flags guiding the way vigorously waved to clear a path. Far away, the top of a large flag appeared, the sound getting closer and closer.

"The fair is really coming now," A-Gen said.

A-Mao's small eyes darted around for a moment, and he nodded, chewing melon seeds and carefully holding onto the crossbar of the short railing. (To be continued)

New Song Introduction 233

The Song of Böel

Lyrics by Tian Han, composed by Lü Ji

Life is like a turbid river of mud, flowing slowly and steadily towards who knows where. Machines have dried up our muscles and bones, coal smoke has blackened our faces, and liquor has burned our hearts. The resentment that builds up in our hearts after drinking cannot find a place to vent, so we are helpless. Every day we see fathers beating their sons, husbands beating their wives, or else we pick up a prostitute and dance a jig! Or we pick up an accordion and sing a sweet love song:

"Life is just a few decades, and it's all the same. My love, my love, why aren't you happy? Why aren't you happy, my love?"

Storytelling 235

He Yunfang and He Qinfang's Double Pearl Phoenix

Sino-Western (1040) 1:00 PM to 1:45 PM

Opening Chapter "Tea Visit"

He was honest and upright throughout his life. He was Fan Zhongyan, a famous minister of the Song Dynasty. He was newly appointed as the prefect of Kaifeng. He privately inspected the people. Disguised as a traveler, who could recognize him? He leisurely strolled into a teahouse. Holding fragrant tea, he sipped the clear spring water. Suddenly, he saw a master and servant walking in. Inside, the teahouse owner greeted them obsequiously, repeatedly calling him "Master." After a few words, they disagreed and acted rudely. Before leaving, they uttered vicious words. He inquired and learned that Zhang Min was evil, acting lawlessly and relying on his wealth and power. His family slave helped him do evil, calling Zhang Qian. He tried to talk about Zhou Yu's affair in the past, who used his desire for beauty to take a life. He filled two ingots of silver with twenty ingots, poisoning the scholar and exiling him. They parted ways at the government office, with tears streaming down their faces. His wife and children were scattered for more than ten years. After investigating the evil deeds, he returned to the yamen. He immediately issued a judgment on the spot. Zhang Min's crimes were unforgivable. The high-hanging mirror shone impartially. Ten thousand people admired and praised the pure sky.

Paul Muni's "Human Hell" Scene After "The Fugitive" 241

Shanghai Grand Theater Screening

Silver Scribbles 242

— Zhi Er

Ruth has returned to China from Japan (staying for a while and reportedly going to Japan again). Yesterday, when "Human Hell" was being tested at the Shanghai Grand Theater, she appeared. Acquaintances were surprised to ask, "When did you come back?" She said lightly, "More than a month ago, I've been in the countryside. I haven't been in Shanghai for a few days."

"Human Hell" exposed some of the dark conditions of prisons, which made people quite moved, but there were places similar to "The Fugitive" and "Raging Tide", but it was not as calm and powerful as "The Fugitive" and "Raging Tide". The play itself cannot be considered wrong, and several comparisons are very powerful. There are sudden places throughout the film, I don't know if it was cut or was originally like this?

There is such a saying: The "Resurrection" of the Nanjing China Stage Association originally wanted to invite a female musician who had studied in France to star, but because she was not a "star" and was afraid that there would not be much appeal when it was released, it was decided to call Hu Ping to star.

A friend said with great emotion after hearing this news: "The star system has its reasons for existence after all."
